+0000https://scipython.com/blog/the-m%C3%B6bius-function-and-the-mertens-conjecture/Featured on frontpageSearching for pi-mnemonic strings in a texthttps://scipython.com/blog/searching-for-pi-mnemonic-strings-in-a-text/<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piphilology">Piphilology</a> comprises the creation and use of mnemonic techniques to remember a span of digits of the mathematical constant $\pi$. One famous technique, attributed to the physicist <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Jeans">James Jeans</a> uses the number of letters in each word of the sentence:</p> <blockquote> <p>How I want a drink, alcoholic of course, after the heavy chapters involving quantum mechanics </p> </blockquote> <p>The Python script below takes a text file on the command line and tries to find the longest sequence (``run") of words that could serve as a mnemonic. It turns out that random texts don't tend to contain mnemonics of any useful length, and a typical novel of a few hundred thousand words will likely not contain one of length longer than 5 words. For example, using the text of <em>David Copperfield</em> from <a href="https://www.gutenberg.org/">Project Gutenberg</a>:</p> <div class="codehilite"><pre><span></span>$ python get_pi_mnemonic.py david-copperfield.txt david-copperfield.txt contains <span class="m">357732</span> words <span class="m">898</span> runs of length <span class="m">3</span> found: <span class="s2">"XIX I Look"</span>, <span class="s2">"XXX A Loss"</span>, <span class="s2">"Yet I have"</span>, <span class="s2">"all I know"</span>, <span class="s2">"had a sure"</span>, <span class="s2">"her I dare"</span>, <span class="s2">"not a rook"</span>, <span class="s2">"yes I fear"</span>, ... <span class="m">32</span> runs of length <span class="m">4</span> found: <span class="s2">"III I Have a"</span>, <span class="s2">"you I have a"</span>, <span class="s2">"And I hope I"</span>, <span class="s2">"man I have a"</span>, <span class="s2">"her a turn I"</span>, <span class="s2">"sir I said I"</span>, <span class="s2">"sir I said I"</span>, <span class="s2">"Yes I said I"</span>, ... <span class="m">5</span> runs of length <span class="m">5</span> found: <span class="s2">"and I made a cloak"</span>, <span class="s2">"you I hope I shall"</span>, <span class="s2">"was a lady I think"</span>, <span class="s2">"now a mans a judge"</span>, <span class="s2">"why I felt a vague"</span> </pre></div> <p>The sixth digit of $\pi$ is 9 and there aren't that many common words with 9 letters (compared to those with 1Â â€“Â 5 letters).</p> <div class="codehilite"><pre><span></span><span class="n">bar</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nb">range</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">0</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">max_word_len</span><span class="o">+</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">),</span> <span class="n">f</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="n">plt</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">xlim</span><span <span class="n">plt</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">xlabel</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s1">'Word length'</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="n">plt</span><span class="n">plt</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">show</span><span class="p">()</span> </pre></div>christianThu, 19 Dec 2019 08:59:42 +0000https://scipython.com/blog/searching-for-pi-mnemonic-strings-in-a-text/Quadtrees #1: Backgroundhttps://scipython.com/blog/quadtree-1-background/<p>The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadtree">quadtree</a> data structure is a convenient way to store the location of arbitrarily-distributed points in two-dimensional space. Quadtrees are often used in image processing and collision detection.</p> <p>A quadtree works by recursively partitioning a two-dimensional space into regions represented by nodes which are allowed to hold a maximum number of distinct points (these could be particles in a molecular dynamics simulation, for example). In constructing the quadtree by inserting points, if a node becomes full, it acquires (becomes the parent of) four further nodes by splitting its region into (usually equally-sized) subregions. A new point falling within the original region can then be assigned to the appropriate subregion.</p> <p>Quadtrees are used in algorithms that can, for example, identify particle collisions, locate all the points in a given area, or find the nearest point to a provided location. The structure of the quadtree means that points that are far from the area or point being considered do not need to be compared.</p> <p>The following figures illustrate the working of a quadtree with a maximum number of 3 items per node.</p> <p>Initially, the root node, A, may be filled with three items:</p> <p><img alt="quadtree-1" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/quadtree/quadtree-1.png"/></p> <p>Adding a further item must cause the node to branch, creating four new child nodes, Bâ€“E. The location of the item means it is placed in node C:</p> <p><img alt="quadtree-2" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/quadtree/quadtree-2.png"/></p> <p>The next three items happen to end up in node E, which can hold them without branching:</p> <p><img alt="quadtree-3" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/quadtree/quadtree-3.png"/></p> <p>Similarly, the next three happen to fall into node D:</p> <p><img alt="quadtree-4" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/quadtree/quadtree-4.png"/></p> <p>The 11th point also falls into the domain of node D, which must branch into four new nodes, Fâ€“I. The point is assigned to node F:</p> <p><img alt="quadtree-5" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/quadtree/quadtree-5.png"/></p>christianFri, 15 Nov 2019 16:12:21 +0000https://scipython.com/blog/quadtree-1-background/Processing UK Ordnance Survey terrain datahttps://scipython.com/blog/processing-uk-ordnance-survey-terrain-data/<p>The UK's <a href="https://www.ordnancesurvey.co.uk/">Ordnance Survey</a> mapping agency now makes its 50 m resolution elevation data freely-available through its online <a href="https://www.ordnancesurvey.co.uk/opendatadownload/products.html#TERR50">OpenData download service</a>. This article uses Python, NumPy and Matplotlib to process and visualize these data without using a specialized GIS library.</p> <p><img alt="enter image description here" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/terrain50/os-uk.png"/></p> <p>First create a directory, such as <code>terrain50/</code>, and within it create a second directory, <code>data/</code>. From the <a href="https://www.ordnancesurvey.co.uk/opendatadownload/products.html#TERR50">OpenData website</a> select ASCII Grid and GML (Grid) and download the archive and unzip it to <code>terr50_gagg_gb/</code> inside <code>data/</code>. This unzipped archive contains further zipped data files containing <code>.asc</code> files named <code>XX##.asc</code> where <code>XX</code> is a two-letter code identifying each 100 km Ã— 100 km grid sq of the OS map and <code>##</code> identifies the 10 km Ã— 10 km grid squares within this larger square. For more on the OS grid system, see <a href="https://getoutside.ordnancesurvey.co.uk/guides/beginners-guide-to-grid-references/">this Beginner's Guide</a>.</p> <p>From within <code>terrain50/data/</code>, unzip all the files with</p> <div class="codehilite"><pre><span></span>find . -name "*.zip" -exec unzip '{}' ';' </pre></div> <p>(On a Mac / Unix / Linux system; on Windows do ... something else). You can now delete the archive, <code>terr50_gagg_gb/</code> directory and any file that doesn't end in <code>.asc</code> to save space.</p> <p>It will be convenient to extract all the elevation data class="o">.</span><span class="n">fill_between</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">x</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">row</span><span class="o">+</span><span </pre></div>christianThu, 10 Oct 2019 11:45:25 +0000https://scipython.com/blog/processing-uk-ordnance-survey-terrain-data/Featured on frontpageVisualizing uncertainties in plotted datahttps://scipython.com/blog/visualizing-uncertainties-in-plotted-data/<p>The equation for the temperature-dependence of the diffusion of hydrogen in tungsten may be written in Arrhenius form: $$ k = A\exp\left(-\frac{E}{T}\right) \quad \Rightarrow \; \ln k = \ln A - \frac{E}{T}, $$ where the temperature, $T$, and activation energy, $E$, are expressed in eV and the pre-exponential Arrhenius parameter, $A$, and rate constant, $k$, take units of $\mathrm{m^2\,s^{-1}}$. From the study of Frauenfelder [1] the parameters $A$ and $E$ may be associated with uncertainties as follows: $$ \begin{align*} A &amp; = (4.1 \pm 0.5) \times 10^{-7}\;\mathrm{m^2\,s^{-1}}, \\ E &amp;= 0.39 \pm 0.08 \;\mathrm{eV}. \end{align*} $$ These uncertainties can be propagated to the expression for $\ln k$: $$ \sigma_{\ln k} \approx \sqrt{ \left( \frac{\sigma_A}{A} \right)^2 + \left( \frac{\sigma_E}{T} \right)^2 }. $$ If we assume the uncertainty remains normally-distributed, Matplotlib's <code>imshow</code> function can be used to illustrate the Arrhenius equation for this data.</p> <p><img alt="enter image description here" class="fig" src="https://scipython.com/static/media/uploads/blog/rate-eqn-unc/plot-rate-unc.png"/></p> <p>The following code produces the above plot</p> <div class="codehilite"><pre><span></span><span class="kn">import</span> <span class="nn">numpy</span> <span class="kn">as</span> <span class="nn">np</span> <span class="kn">from</span> <span class="nn">scipy.constants</span> <span class="kn">import</span> <span class="n">k</span> <span class="k">as</span> <span class="n">kB</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">e</span> <span class="kn">import</span> <span class="nn">matplotlib.pyplot</span> <span class="kn">as</span> <span class="nn">plt</span> <span class="kn">from</span> <span class="nn">matplotlib</span> <span class="kn">import</span> <span class="n">rc</span> <span class="n">rc</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s1">'font'</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="o">**</span><span class="p">{</span><span class="s1">'family'</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="s1">'serif'</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="s1">'serif'</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="p">[</span><span class="s1">'Computer Modern'</span><span class="p">],</span> <span class="s1">'size'</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="mi">14</span><span class="p">})</span> <span Equally-spaced inverse temperature grid, K^-1</span> <span class="n">invT</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="n">np</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">linspace</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="n">Tmax</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">1</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="n">Tmin</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">nT</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="c1"># Convert to eV^-1</span> <span class="n">invTeV</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="n">e</span> <span class="o">*</span> <span class="n">invT</span> <span class="o">/</span> <span class="n">kB</span> <span class="c1"># Activation energy (eV), Arrhenius parameter (m2.s-1) and their</span> <span class="c1"># 1-sigma uncertainties</span> <span class="n">E</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">A</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="mf">0.39</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mf">4.1e-7</span> <span class="n">E_unc</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">A_unc</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="mf">0.08</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mf">0.5e-7</span> <span class="k">def</span> <span class="nf">calc_lnk</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">A</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">E</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">invTeV</span><span class="p">):</span> <span class="sd">"""Calculate the log of the rate coefficient."""</span> <span class="k">return</span> <span class="n">np</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">log</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">A</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="o">-</span> <span class="n">E</span> <span <span class="c1"># Propagate the errors in A, E to the error in ln(k).
